<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_shuttles_Atlantis_(STS-125)_and_Endeavour_(STS-400)_on_launch_pads.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2968" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg/220px-Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg/330px-Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg/440px-Space_shuttles_Atlantis_%28STS-125%29_and_Endeavour_%28STS-400%29_on_launch_pads.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_shuttles_Atlantis_(STS-125)_and_Endeavour_(STS-400)_on_launch_pads.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Atlantis</i> and <i>Endeavour</i> on launch pads. This particular occasion is due to the final Hubble servicing mission, where the International Space Station is unreachable, which necessitates having a Shuttle on standby for a possible rescue mission.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Orbiter</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tech_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-tech-65">[65]</a></sup> (for <i>Endeavour</i>, OV-105)</p>
<ul>
<li>Length: 122.17 ft (37.237 m)</li>
<li>Wingspan: 78.06 ft (23.79 m)</li>
<li>Height: 56.58 ft (17.25 m)</li>
<li>Empty weight: 172,000 lb (78,000 kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gross liftoff weight (Orbiter only): 240,000 lb (110,000 kg)</li>
<li>Maximum landing weight: 230,000 lb (100,000 kg)</li>
<li>Payload to Landing (Return Payload): 32,000 lb (14,400 kg)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woodcock_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-woodcock-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li>Maximum payload: 55,250 lb (25,060 kg)</li>
<li>Payload to LEO (204 kilometers (110 nmi) @ 28.5° <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">inclination</a>: 27,500 kilograms (60,600 lb)</li>
<li>Payload to LEO (407 kilometers (220 nmi) @ 51.6° to ISS): 16,050 kilograms (35,380 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>Payload to GTO: 8,390 lb (3,806 kg)</li>
<li>Payload to Polar Orbit: 28,000 lb (12,700 kg)</li>
<li>Note launch payloads modified by External Tank (ET) choice (ET, LWT, or SLWT)</li>
<li>Payload bay dimensions: 15 by 59 ft (4.6 by 18 m) (diameter by length)</li>
<li>Operational altitude: 100 to 520 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nmi</a> (190 to 960 km; 120 to 600 mi)</li>
<li>Speed: 7,743 m/s (27,870 km/h; 17,320 mph)</li>
<li>Crossrange: 1,085 nmi (2,009 km; 1,249 mi)</li>
<li>Main Stage (SSME with external tank)
<ul>
<li>Engines: Three Rocketdyne Block II SSMEs, each with a sea level <a href="/wiki/Thrust" title="Thrust">thrust</a> of 393,800 lbf (1,752 kN) at 104% power</li>
<li>Thrust (at liftoff, sea level, 104% power, all 3 engines): 1,181,400 lbf (5,255 kN)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Specific_impulse" title="Specific impulse">Specific impulse</a>: 455 seconds (4.46 km/s)</li>
<li>Burn time: 480 s</li>
<li>Fuel: Liquid Hydrogen/Liquid Oxygen</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_Maneuvering_System" title="Orbital Maneuvering System">Orbital Maneuvering System</a>
<ul>
<li>Engines: 2 OMS Engines</li>
<li>Thrust: 53.4 kN (12,000 lbf) combined total vacuum thrust</li>
<li>Specific impulse: 316 seconds (3.10 km/s)</li>
<li>Burn time: 150–250 s typical burn; 1250 s deorbit burn</li>
<li>Fuel: MMH/N2O4</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Crew: Varies.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>The earliest Shuttle flights had the minimum crew of two; many later missions a crew of five. By program end, typically seven people would fly: (<a href="/wiki/Commander" title="Commander">commander</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aviator" title="Aviator">pilot</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Mission_specialist" title="Mission specialist">mission specialists</a>, one of whom (MS-2) acted as the <a href="/wiki/Flight_engineer" title="Flight engineer">flight engineer</a> starting with STS-9 in 1983). On two occasions, eight astronauts have flown (<a href="/wiki/STS-61-A" title="STS-61-A">STS-61-A</a>, <a href="/wiki/STS-71" title="STS-71">STS-71</a>). Eleven people could be accommodated in an emergency mission (see <a href="/wiki/STS-3xx" title="STS-3xx">STS-3xx</a>).</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p><b>External tank</b> (for SLWT)</p>
<ul>
<li>Length: 46.9 m (153.8 ft)</li>
<li>Diameter: 8.4 m (27.6 ft)</li>
<li>Propellant volume: 2,025 m<sup>3</sup> (534,900 U.S. gal)</li>
<li>Empty weight: 26,535 kg (58,500 lb)</li>
<li>Gross liftoff weight (for tank): 756,000 kg (1,670,000 lb)</li>
</ul>
<p><b>Solid Rocket Boosters</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Length: 45.46 m (149 ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_3rd_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_3rd-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>Diameter: 3.71 m (12.2 ft)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_3rd_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_3rd-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>Empty weight (each): 68,000 kg (150,000 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_3rd_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins_3rd-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>Gross liftoff weight (each): 571,000 kg (1,260,000 lb)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STS_prop_systems_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-STS_prop_systems-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>Thrust (at liftoff, sea level, each): 12,500 <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">kN</a> (2,800,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pound-force" title="Pound-force">lbf</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Accid_Report_D.7_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Accid_Report_D.7-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>Specific impulse: 269 seconds (2.64 km/s)</li>
<li>Burn time: 124 s</li>
</ul>
<p><b>System Stack</b></p>
<ul>
<li>Height: 56 m (180 ft)</li>
<li>Gross liftoff weight: 2,000,000 kg (4,400,000 lb)</li>
<li>Total liftoff thrust: 30,160 kN (6,780,000 lbf)</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mission profile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_preparation">Launch preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Launch preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Space_shuttle_launch_countdown" title="Space shuttle launch countdown">Space shuttle launch countdown</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle_launch_commit_criteria" title="Space shuttle launch commit criteria">Space shuttle launch commit criteria</a></div>
<p>All Space Shuttle missions were launched from Kennedy Space Center (KSC). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_shuttle_launch_commit_criteria" title="Space shuttle launch commit criteria">weather criteria used for launch</a> included, but were not limited to: precipitation, temperatures, cloud cover, lightning forecast, wind, and humidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather_launch_criteria_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-weather_launch_criteria-70">[70]</a></sup> The Shuttle was not launched under conditions where it could have been struck by <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>. Aircraft are often struck by lightning with no adverse effects because the electricity of the strike is dissipated through its conductive structure and the aircraft is not electrically <a href="/wiki/Ground_(electricity)" title="Ground (electricity)">grounded</a>. Like most jet airliners, the Shuttle was mainly constructed of conductive aluminum, which would normally shield and protect the internal systems. However, upon liftoff the Shuttle sent out a long exhaust plume as it ascended, and this plume could have triggered lightning by providing a current path to ground. The NASA Anvil Rule for a Shuttle launch stated that an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anvil_cloud" title="Anvil cloud">anvil cloud</a> could not appear within a distance of 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nautical_miles" title="Nautical miles">nautical miles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The Shuttle Launch Weather Officer monitored conditions until the final decision to scrub a launch was announced. In addition, the weather conditions had to be acceptable at one of the Transatlantic Abort Landing sites (one of several <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_abort_modes" title="Space Shuttle abort modes">Space Shuttle abort modes</a>) to launch as well as the solid rocket booster recovery area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather_launch_criteria_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-weather_launch_criteria-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> While the Shuttle might have safely endured a lightning strike, a <a href="/wiki/Apollo_12#Mission_highlights" title="Apollo 12">similar strike caused problems on Apollo 12</a>, so for safety <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> chose not to launch the Shuttle if lightning was possible (NPR8715.5).</p>
<p>Historically, the Shuttle was not launched if its flight would run from December to January (a year-end rollover or YERO). Its flight software, designed in the 1970s, was not designed for this, and would require the orbiter's computers be reset through a change of year, which could cause a glitch while in orbit. In 2007, NASA engineers devised a solution so Shuttle flights could cross the year-end boundary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YERO_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-YERO-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch">Launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the final hold in the countdown at T-minus 9 minutes, the Shuttle went through its final preparations for launch, and the countdown was automatically controlled by the Ground Launch Sequencer (GLS), software at the Launch Control Center, which stopped the count if it sensed a critical problem with any of the Shuttle's onboard systems. The GLS handed off the count to the Shuttle's on-board computers at T minus 31 seconds, in a process called auto sequence start.</p>
<p>At T-minus 16 seconds, the massive sound suppression system (SPS) began to drench the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Launcher_Platform" title="Mobile Launcher Platform">Mobile Launcher Platform</a> (MLP) and SRB trenches with 300,000 US gallons (1,100 m<sup>3</sup>) of water to protect the Orbiter from damage by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acoustical" title="Acoustical">acoustical</a> energy and rocket exhaust reflected from the flame trench and MLP during lift off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sps_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-sps-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>At T-minus 10 seconds, hydrogen igniters were activated under each engine bell to quell the stagnant gas inside the cones before ignition. Failure to burn these gases could trip the onboard sensors and create the possibility of an overpressure and explosion of the vehicle during the firing phase. The main engine turbopumps also began charging the combustion chambers with liquid hydrogen and liquid oxygen at this time. The computers reciprocated this action by allowing the redundant computer systems to begin the firing phase.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2329" data-file-width="3000" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/260px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/390px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg/520px-020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:020408_STS110_Atlantis_launch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Space Shuttle Main Engine ignition</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The three main engines (SSMEs) started at <i>T</i>-6.6 seconds. The main engines ignited sequentially via the Shuttle's general purpose computers (GPCs) at 120 millisecond intervals. All three SSMEs were required to reach 90% rated thrust within three seconds, otherwise the onboard computers would initiate an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RSLS_Abort" title="RSLS Abort">RSLS abort</a>. If all three engines indicated nominal performance by <i>T</i>-3 seconds, they were commanded to gimbal to liftoff configuration and the command would be issued to arm the SRBs for ignition at <i>T</i>-0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-countdown101_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-countdown101-76">[76]</a></sup> Between <i>T</i>-6.6 seconds and <i>T</i>-3 seconds, while the SSMEs were firing but the SRBs were still bolted to the pad, the offset thrust caused the entire launch stack (boosters, tank and orbiter) to pitch down 650 mm (25.5 in) measured at the tip of the external tank. The three second delay after confirmation of SSME operation was to allow the stack to return to nearly vertical. At <i>T</i>-0 seconds, the 8 <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic_fastener" title="Pyrotechnic fastener">frangible nuts</a> holding the SRBs to the pad were detonated, the SSMEs were commanded to 100% throttle, and the SRBs were ignited. By <i>T</i>+0.23 seconds, the SRBs built up enough thrust for liftoff to commence, and reached maximum chamber pressure by <i>T</i>+0.6 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Space_Center" title="Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center" title="Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> assumed control of the flight once the SRBs had cleared the launch tower.</p>
<p>Shortly after liftoff, the Shuttle's main engines were throttled up to 104.5% and the vehicle began a combined roll, pitch and yaw maneuver that placed it onto the correct heading (azimuth) for the planned orbital inclination and in a heads down attitude with wings level. The Shuttle flew upside down during the ascent phase. This orientation allowed a trim angle of attack that was favorable for aerodynamic loads during the region of high dynamic pressure, resulting in a net positive load factor, as well as providing the flight crew with a view of the horizon as a visual reference. The vehicle climbed in a progressively flattening arc, accelerating as the mass of the SRBs and main tank decreased. To achieve low orbit requires much more horizontal than vertical acceleration. This was not visually obvious, since the vehicle rose vertically and was out of sight for most of the horizontal acceleration. The near circular orbital velocity at the 380 kilometers (236 mi) altitude of the International Space Station is 27,650 km/h (17,180 mph), roughly equivalent to Mach 23 at sea level. As the International Space Station orbits at an inclination of 51.6 degrees, missions going there must set orbital inclination to the same value in order to rendezvous with the station.</p>
<p>Around 30 seconds into ascent, the SSMEs were throttled down—usually to 72%, though this varied—to reduce the maximum aerodynamic forces acting on the Shuttle at a point called <a href="/wiki/Max_Q" title="Max Q">Max Q</a>. Additionally, the propellant grain design of the SRBs caused their thrust to drop by about 30% by 50 seconds into ascent. Once the Orbiter's guidance verified that Max Q would be within Shuttle structural limits, the main engines were throttled back up to 104.5%; this throttling down and back up was called the "thrust bucket". To maximize performance, the throttle level and timing of the thrust bucket was shaped to bring the Shuttle as close to aerodynamic limits as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2799" data-file-width="2224" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/220px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/330px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg/440px-STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-1_The_Shuttle%27s_Solid_Rocket_Boosters_break_away_from_Columbia%27s_External_Tank.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Solid Rocket Booster (SRB) separation during STS-1. The white external tank pictured was used on STS-1 and STS-2.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At around <i>T</i>+126 seconds, <a href="/wiki/Pyrotechnic_fastener" title="Pyrotechnic fastener">pyrotechnic fasteners</a> released the SRBs and small separation rockets pushed them laterally away from the vehicle. The SRBs parachuted back to the ocean to be reused. The Shuttle then began accelerating to orbit on the main engines. Acceleration at this point would typically fall to .9 <i><a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g</a></i>, and the vehicle would take on a somewhat nose-up angle to the horizon – it used the main engines to gain and then maintain altitude while it accelerated horizontally towards orbit. At about five and three-quarter minutes into ascent, the orbiter's direct communication links with the ground began to fade, at which point it rolled heads up to reroute its communication links to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite" title="Tracking and Data Relay Satellite">Tracking and Data Relay Satellite</a> system.</p>
<p>At about seven and a half minutes into ascent, the mass of the vehicle was low enough that the engines had to be throttled back to limit vehicle acceleration to 3 <i>g</i> (29.4 m/s² or 96.5 ft/s², equivalent to accelerating from zero to 105.9 km/h (65.8 mph) in a second). The Shuttle would maintain this acceleration for the next minute, and main engine cut-off (MECO) occurred at about eight and a half minutes after launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The main engines were shut down before complete depletion of propellant, as running dry would have destroyed the engines. The oxygen supply was terminated before the hydrogen supply, as the SSMEs reacted unfavorably to other shutdown modes. (Liquid oxygen has a tendency to react violently, and supports combustion when it encounters hot engine metal.) A few seconds after MECO, the external tank was released by firing pyrotechnic fasteners.</p>
<p>At this point the Shuttle and external tank were on a slightly suborbital trajectory, coasting up towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a>. Once at apogee, about half an hour after MECO, the Shuttle's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_Maneuvering_System" title="Orbital Maneuvering System">Orbital Maneuvering System</a> (OMS) engines were fired to raise its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> and achieve orbit, while the external tank fell back into the atmosphere and burned up over the Indian Ocean or the Pacific Ocean depending on launch profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tech_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-tech-65">[65]</a></sup> The sealing action of the tank plumbing and lack of pressure relief systems on the external tank helped it break up in the lower atmosphere. After the foam burned away during re-entry, the heat caused a pressure buildup in the remaining liquid oxygen and hydrogen until the tank exploded. This ensured that any pieces that fell back to Earth were small.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Ascent tracking</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1021" data-file-width="1536" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg/220px-Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg/330px-Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg/440px-Contraves-Goerz_Kineto_Tracking_Mount.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-131_afterglow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-131_afterglow.jpg/220px-STS-131_afterglow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-131_afterglow.jpg/330px-STS-131_afterglow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-131_afterglow.jpg/440px-STS-131_afterglow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-131_afterglow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Multicolored <a href="/wiki/Afterglow" title="Afterglow">afterglow</a> of the <a href="/wiki/STS-131" title="STS-131">STS-131</a> launch</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Shuttle was monitored throughout its ascent for short range tracking (10 seconds before liftoff through 57 seconds after), medium range (7 seconds before liftoff through 110 seconds after) and long range (7 seconds before liftoff through 165 seconds after). Short range cameras included 22 16mm cameras on the Mobile Launch Platform and 8 16mm on the Fixed Service Structure, 4 high speed fixed cameras located on the perimeter of the launch complex plus an additional 42 fixed cameras with 16mm motion picture film. Medium range cameras included remotely operated tracking cameras at the launch complex plus 6 sites along the immediate coast north and south of the launch pad, each with 800mm lens and high speed cameras running 100 frames per second. These cameras ran for only 4–10 seconds due to limitations in the amount of film available. Long range cameras included those mounted on the external tank, SRBs and orbiter itself which streamed live video back to the ground providing valuable information about any debris falling during ascent. Long range tracking cameras with 400-inch film and 200-inch video lenses were operated by a photographer at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playalinda_Beach" title="Playalinda Beach">Playalinda Beach</a> as well as 9 other sites from 38 miles north at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponce_Inlet" title="Ponce Inlet">Ponce Inlet</a> to 23 miles south to <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Air_Force_Base" title="Patrick Air Force Base">Patrick Air Force Base</a> (PAFB) and additional mobile optical tracking camera was stationed on Merritt Island during launches. A total of 10 HD cameras were used both for ascent information for engineers and broadcast feeds to networks such as <a href="/wiki/NASA_TV" title="NASA TV">NASA TV</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDNet" title="HDNet">HDNet</a>. The number of cameras significantly increased and numerous existing cameras were upgraded at the recommendation of the <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Accident_Investigation_Board" title="Columbia Accident Investigation Board">Columbia Accident Investigation Board</a> to provide better information about the debris during launch. Debris was also tracked using a pair of <a href="/wiki/Weibel_Scientific" title="Weibel Scientific">Weibel</a> Continuous Pulse Doppler X-band radars, one on board the SRB recovery ship <a href="/wiki/MV_Liberty_Star" title="MV Liberty Star">MV <i>Liberty Star</i></a> positioned north east of the launch pad and on a ship positioned south of the launch pad. Additionally, during the first 2 flights following the loss of <i>Columbia</i> and her crew, a pair of NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WB-57" title="WB-57">WB-57</a> reconnaissance aircraft equipped with HD Video and Infrared flew at 60,000 feet (18,000 m) to provide additional views of the launch ascent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Kennedy Space Center also invested nearly $3 million in improvements to the digital video analysis systems in support of debris tracking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_orbit">In orbit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: In orbit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Once in orbit, the Shuttle usually flew at an altitude of 320 km (170 nmi) and occasionally as high as 650 km (350 nmi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In the 1980s and 1990s, many flights involved space science missions on the NASA/ESA Spacelab, or launching various types of satellites and science probes. By the 1990s and 2000s the focus shifted more to servicing the space station, with fewer satellite launches. Most missions involved staying in orbit several days to two weeks, although longer missions were possible with the <a href="/wiki/Extended_Duration_Orbiter" title="Extended Duration Orbiter">Extended Duration Orbiter</a> add-on or when attached to a space station.</p>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6048" data-file-width="4032" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/220px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/330px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/440px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Endeavour</i> docked at ISS</div>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-entry_and_landing">Re-entry and landing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Re-entry and landing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(June 2007)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Almost the entire Space Shuttle re-entry procedure, except for lowering the landing gear and deploying the air data probes, was normally performed under computer control. However, the re-entry could be flown entirely manually if an emergency arose. The approach and landing phase could be controlled by the autopilot, but was usually hand flown.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21,_2011,_to_its_final_landing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/220px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/330px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg/440px-Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21%2C_2011%2C_to_its_final_landing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_in_the_sky_on_July_21,_2011,_to_its_final_landing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Glowing plasma trail from Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> re-entry as seen from the Space Station</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The vehicle began re-entry by firing the Orbital maneuvering system engines, while flying upside down, backside first, in the opposite direction to orbital motion for approximately three minutes, which reduced the Shuttle's velocity by about 200 mph (322 km/h). The resultant slowing of the Shuttle lowered its orbital <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a> down into the upper atmosphere. The Shuttle then flipped over, by pushing its nose down (which was actually "up" relative to the Earth, because it was flying upside down). This OMS firing was done roughly halfway around the globe from the landing site.</p>
<p>The vehicle started encountering more significant air density in the lower thermosphere at about 400,000 ft (120 km), at around <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a> 25, 8,200 m/s (30,000 km/h; 18,000 mph). The vehicle was controlled by a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_Control_System" title="Reaction Control System">RCS thrusters</a> and control surfaces, to fly at a 40-degree nose-up attitude, producing high drag, not only to slow it down to landing speed, but also to reduce reentry heating. As the vehicle encountered progressively denser air, it began a gradual transition from spacecraft to aircraft. In a straight line, its 40-degree nose-up attitude would cause the descent angle to flatten-out, or even rise. The vehicle therefore performed a series of four steep S-shaped banking turns, each lasting several minutes, at up to 70 degrees of bank, while still maintaining the 40-degree angle of attack. In this way it dissipated speed sideways rather than upwards. This occurred during the 'hottest' phase of re-entry, when the heat-shield glowed red and the G-forces were at their highest. By the end of the last turn, the transition to aircraft was almost complete. The vehicle leveled its wings, lowered its nose into a shallow dive and began its approach to the landing site.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">

</div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:27px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg/120px-Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg/180px-Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg/240px-Nasa_Shuttle_Test_Using_Electron_Beam_full.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:30px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CFD_Shuttle.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="516" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CFD_Shuttle.jpg/120px-CFD_Shuttle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CFD_Shuttle.jpg/180px-CFD_Shuttle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/CFD_Shuttle.jpg/240px-CFD_Shuttle.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A computer simulation of high velocity air flow around the Space Shuttle during re-entry.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The orbiter's maximum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glide_ratio" title="Glide ratio">glide ratio</a>/<a href="/wiki/Lift-to-drag_ratio" title="Lift-to-drag ratio">lift-to-drag ratio</a> varies considerably with speed, ranging from 1:1 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypersonic" title="Hypersonic">hypersonic</a> speeds, 2:1 at supersonic speeds and reaching 4.5:1 at subsonic speeds during approach and landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the lower atmosphere, the orbiter flies much like a conventional glider, except for a much higher descent rate, over 50 m/s (180 km/h; 110 mph) or 9,800 fpm. At approximately Mach 3, two air data probes, located on the left and right sides of the orbiter's forward lower fuselage, are deployed to sense air pressure related to the vehicle's movement in the atmosphere.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Final approach and landing phase</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:712px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Sts-127_landing.ogv/712px--Sts-127_landing.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:712px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="216.40997732426" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Sts-127_landing.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Sts-127_landing.ogv" type="video/ogg; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sts-127_landing.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/STS-127" title="STS-127">STS-127</a>, Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i> landing video, 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When the approach and landing phase began, the orbiter was at a 3,000 m (9,800 ft) altitude, 12 km (7.5 mi) from the runway. The pilots applied aerodynamic braking to help slow down the vehicle. The orbiter's speed was reduced from 682 to 346 km/h (424 to 215 mph), approximately, at touch-down (compared to 260 km/h or 160 mph for a jet airliner). The landing gear was deployed while the Orbiter was flying at 430 km/h (270 mph). To assist the speed brakes, a 12 m (39 ft) drag chute was deployed either after main gear or nose gear touchdown (depending on selected chute deploy mode) at about 343 km/h (213 mph). The chute was jettisoned once the orbiter slowed to 110 km/h (68.4 mph).</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:35px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concluding_the_STS-133_mission,_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1996" data-file-width="3000" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg/120px-Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg/180px-Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg/240px-Concluding_the_STS-133_mission%2C_Space_Shuttle_Discovery_touches_down_at_the_Shuttle_Landing_Facility.jpg 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>

</div>

</li>

</ul>
<p><small><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/18px-Commons-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/24px-Commons-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/></a> Media related to <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Landings_of_space_shuttles" title="commons:Category:Landings of space shuttles">Landings of space Shuttles</a> at Wikimedia Commons</small></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-landing_processing">Post-landing processing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Post-landing processing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Processing_Facility" title="Orbiter Processing Facility">Orbiter Processing Facility</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1416" data-file-width="1898" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/220px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/330px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg/440px-Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Discovery_mission_completed_q.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Discovery</i> after landing on Earth for crew disembarkment</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After landing, the vehicle stayed on the runway for several hours for the orbiter to cool. Teams at the front and rear of the orbiter tested for presence of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monomethylhydrazine" title="Monomethylhydrazine">monomethylhydrazine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_tetroxide" title="Nitrogen tetroxide">nitrogen tetroxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (fuels and by-products of the reaction control system and the orbiter's three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxiliary_Power_Unit" title="Auxiliary Power Unit">APUs</a>). If hydrogen was detected, an emergency would be declared, the orbiter powered down and teams would evacuate the area. A convoy of 25 specially designed vehicles and 150 trained engineers and technicians approached the orbiter. Purge and vent lines were attached to remove toxic gases from fuel lines and the cargo bay about 45–60 minutes after landing. A <a href="/wiki/Flight_surgeon" title="Flight surgeon">flight surgeon</a> boarded the orbiter for initial medical checks of the crew before disembarking. Once the crew left the orbiter, responsibility for the vehicle was handed from the Johnson Space Center back to the Kennedy Space Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afterlandingpao_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-afterlandingpao-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>If the mission ended at <a href="/wiki/Edwards_Air_Force_Base" title="Edwards Air Force Base">Edwards Air Force Base</a> in California, <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Space_Harbor" title="White Sands Space Harbor">White Sands Space Harbor</a> in New Mexico, or any of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_shuttle_landing_runways" title="List of space shuttle landing runways">runways the orbiter might use in an emergency</a>, the orbiter was loaded atop the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Carrier_Aircraft" title="Shuttle Carrier Aircraft">Shuttle Carrier Aircraft</a>, a modified 747, for transport back to the Kennedy Space Center, landing at the <a href="/wiki/Shuttle_Landing_Facility" title="Shuttle Landing Facility">Shuttle Landing Facility</a>. Once at the Shuttle Landing Facility, the orbiter was then towed 2 miles (3.2 km) along a tow-way and access roads normally used by tour buses and KSC employees to the <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Processing_Facility" title="Orbiter Processing Facility">Orbiter Processing Facility</a> where it began a months-long preparation process for the next mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afterlandingpao_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-afterlandingpao-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landing_sites">Landing sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Landing sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_space_shuttle_landing_runways" title="List of space shuttle landing runways">List of space shuttle landing runways</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg/220px-Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg/330px-Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg/440px-Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlantis_is_landing_after_STS-30_mission.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i> deploys the <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a> before landing.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>NASA preferred Space Shuttle landings to be at <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> If weather conditions made landing there unfavorable, the Shuttle could delay its landing until conditions are favorable, touch down at Edwards Air Force Base, California, or use one of the multiple alternate landing sites around the world. A landing at any site other than Kennedy Space Center meant that after touchdown the Shuttle must be mated to the Shuttle Carrier Aircraft and returned to <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>. Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i> (<a href="/wiki/STS-3" title="STS-3">STS-3</a>) once landed at the <a href="/wiki/White_Sands_Space_Harbor" title="White Sands Space Harbor">White Sands Space Harbor</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>; this was viewed as a last resort as NASA scientists believed that the sand could potentially damage the Shuttle's exterior.</p>
<p>There were many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_landing_sites" title="Emergency landing sites">alternative landing sites</a> that were never used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Risk_contributors">Risk contributors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Risk contributors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="425" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg/220px-STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg/330px-STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/df/STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STS-133_docked_to_ISS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Discovery</i> at ISS in 2011 (STS-133)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An example of technical risk analysis for a STS mission is SPRA iteration 3.1 top risk contributors for STS-133:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copv_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-copv-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>Micro-Meteoroid Orbital Debris (MMOD) strikes</li>
<li>Space Shuttle Main Engine (SSME)-induced or SSME catastrophic failure</li>
<li>Ascent debris strikes to TPS leading to LOCV on orbit or entry</li>
<li>Crew error during entry</li>
<li>RSRM-induced RSRM catastrophic failure (RSRM are the rocket motors of the SRBs)</li>
<li>COPV failure (COPV are tanks inside the orbiter that hold gas at high pressure)</li>
</ol>
<p>An internal NASA risk assessment study (conducted by the Shuttle Program Safety and Mission Assurance Office at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Johnson Space Center</a>) released in late 2010 or early 2011 concluded that the agency had seriously underestimated the level of risk involved in operating the Shuttle. The report assessed that there was a 1 in 9 chance of a catastrophic disaster during the first nine flights of the Shuttle but that safety improvements had later improved the risk ratio to 1 in 90.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet_history">Fleet history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Fleet history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Space_Shuttle_missions" title="List of Space Shuttle missions">List of Space Shuttle missions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Shuttle_major_events">Space Shuttle major events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Space_Shuttle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Space Shuttle major events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Space_Shuttle_Atlantis_launches_from_KSC_on_STS-132_side_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
